<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Mackerel</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Mackerel_(disambiguation)" title="Mackerel (disambiguation)">Mackerel (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px">
<div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:;background-color:">Mackerel</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trachurus_declivis.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="736" data-file-width="1024" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Trachurus_declivis.jpg/300px-Trachurus_declivis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Trachurus_declivis.jpg/450px-Trachurus_declivis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Trachurus_declivis.jpg/600px-Trachurus_declivis.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
<center>Some species of mackerel <a href="/wiki/Fish_migration" title="Fish migration">migrate</a> in <a href="/wiki/Shoaling_and_schooling" title="Shoaling and schooling">schools</a> for long distances along the coast and other species cross oceans</center>
</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_capture_of_all_mackerel_1950%E2%80%932009.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="799" data-file-width="1247" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Global_capture_of_all_mackerel_1950%E2%80%932009.png/300px-Global_capture_of_all_mackerel_1950%E2%80%932009.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Global_capture_of_all_mackerel_1950%E2%80%932009.png/450px-Global_capture_of_all_mackerel_1950%E2%80%932009.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Global_capture_of_all_mackerel_1950%E2%80%932009.png/600px-Global_capture_of_all_mackerel_1950%E2%80%932009.png 2x" width="300"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
<center>Global commercial capture of mackerel in million tonnes<br/>
as reported by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FAO" title="FAO">FAO</a> 1950–2009<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOdata_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAOdata-1">[1]</a></sup></center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Mackerel</b> is a common name applied to a number of different species of <a href="/wiki/Pelagic_fish" title="Pelagic fish">pelagic fish</a>, mostly, but not exclusively, from the family <a href="/wiki/Scombridae" title="Scombridae">Scombridae</a>. They are found in both temperate and tropical seas, mostly living along the coast or offshore in the oceanic environment.</p>
<p>Mackerel typically have vertical stripes on their backs and deeply forked tails. Many species are restricted in their distribution ranges, and live in separate populations or <a href="/wiki/Fish_stock" title="Fish stock">fish stocks</a> based on geography. Some stocks <a href="/wiki/Fish_migration" title="Fish migration">migrate</a> in large <a href="/wiki/Shoaling_and_schooling" title="Shoaling and schooling">schools</a> along the coast to suitable spawning grounds, where they spawn in fairly shallow waters. After spawning they return the way they came, in smaller schools, to suitable feeding grounds often near an area of <a href="/wiki/Upwelling" title="Upwelling">upwelling</a>. From there they may move offshore into deeper waters and spend the winter in relative inactivity. Other stocks migrate across oceans.</p>
<p>Smaller mackerel are <a href="/wiki/Forage_fish" title="Forage fish">forage fish</a> for larger predators, including larger mackerel and <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_cod" title="Atlantic cod">Atlantic cod</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Flocks of seabirds, as well as whales, dolphins, sharks and schools of larger fish such as <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marlin" title="Marlin">marlin</a> follow mackerel schools and attack them in sophisticated and cooperative ways. Mackerel is high in <a href="/wiki/Omega-3_fatty_acid" title="Omega-3 fatty acid">omega-3 oils</a> and is intensively harvested by humans. In 2009, over five million tons were landed by commercial fishermen<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOdata_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAOdata-1">[1]</a></sup> (see graph on the right). Sport fishermen value the fighting abilities of the <a href="/wiki/King_mackerel" title="King mackerel">king mackerel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Species">Species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mackerel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Over 30 different species, principally belonging to the family Scombridae, are commonly referred to as mackerel. The term "mackerel" means "marked" or "spotted", and derives from the <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> <i>maquerel</i>, around 1300, meaning a pimp or procurer. The connection is not altogether clear, but mackerel <a href="/wiki/Spawn_(biology)" title="Spawn (biology)">spawn</a> enthusiastically in shoals near the coast, and medieval ideas on animal procreation were creative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scombroid_mackerels">Scombroid mackerels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mackerel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Scombroid mackerels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>About 21 species in the family <a href="/wiki/Scombridae" title="Scombridae">Scombridae</a> are commonly called mackerel. The <a href="/wiki/Type_species" title="Type species">type species</a> for the scombroid mackerel is the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_mackerel" title="Atlantic mackerel">Atlantic mackerel</a>, <i>Scomber scombrus</i>. Until recently, <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_chub_mackerel" title="Atlantic chub mackerel">Atlantic chub mackerel</a> and Indo-Pacific <a href="/wiki/Chub_mackerel" title="Chub mackerel">chub mackerel</a> were thought to be subspecies of the same species. In 1999, Collette established, on molecular and morphological considerations, that these are separate species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCNscombrus_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-IUCNscombrus-5">[5]</a></sup> Mackerel are smaller with shorter lifecycles than their close relatives, the <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a>, which are also members of the same family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Juan-Jorda2011_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Juan-Jorda2011-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>

<p><span id="True_mackerels"></span><span id="True_mackerel"></span><span id="true_mackerel"></span><span id="true_mackerels"></span></p>
<h4><span id="Scombrini,_the_true_mackerels"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Scombrini.2C_the_true_mackerels">Scombrini, the true mackerels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mackerel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Scombrini, the true mackerels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The true mackerels belong to the <a href="/wiki/Tribe_(biology)" title="Tribe (biology)">tribe</a> <a href="/wiki/Scombrini" title="Scombrini">Scombrini</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITIS_Scombrini_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITIS_Scombrini-8">[8]</a></sup> The tribe consists of seven species, each belonging to one of two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genera" title="Genera">genera</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Scomber" title="Scomber">Scomber</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Rastrelliger" title="Rastrelliger">Rastrelliger</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITIS_Scomber_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITIS_Scomber-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITIS_Rastrelliger_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITIS_Rastrelliger-10">[10]</a></sup></p>

<p><span id="Spanish_mackerels"></span><span id="Spanish_mackerel"></span></p>
<h4><span id="Scomberomorini,_the_Spanish_mackerels"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Scomberomorini.2C_the_Spanish_mackerels">Scomberomorini, the Spanish mackerels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mackerel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Scomberomorini, the Spanish mackerels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Spanish_mackerel" title="Spanish mackerel">Spanish mackerels</a> belong to the tribe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scomberomorini" title="Scomberomorini">Scomberomorini</a>, which is the "sister tribe" of the true mackerels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITIS_Scomberomorini_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITIS_Scomberomorini-28">[28]</a></sup> This tribe consists of 21 species in all—18 of those are classified into the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Scomberomorus" title="Scomberomorus">Scomberomorus</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITIS_Scomberomorus_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITIS_Scomberomorus-29">[29]</a></sup> two into <i><a href="/wiki/Grammatorcynus" title="Grammatorcynus">Grammatorcynus</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITIS_Grammatorcynus_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITIS_Grammatorcynus-30">[30]</a></sup> and a single species into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monotypic" title="Monotypic">monotypic</a> genus <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acanthocybium" title="Acanthocybium">Acanthocybium</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITIS_Acanthocybium_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITIS_Acanthocybium-31">[31]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_mackerel">Other mackerel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mackerel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Other mackerel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In addition, a number of species with mackerel-like characteristics in the families <a href="/wiki/Carangidae" title="Carangidae">Carangidae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hexagrammidae" title="Hexagrammidae">Hexagrammidae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gempylidae" title="Gempylidae">Gempylidae</a> are commonly referred to as mackerel. There has been some confusion between the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_jack_mackerel" title="Pacific jack mackerel">Pacific jack mackerel</a> (<i>Trachurus symmetricus</i>) and the heavily harvested <a href="/wiki/Chilean_jack_mackerel" title="Chilean jack mackerel">Chilean jack mackerel</a> (<i>Trachurus murphyi</i>). These have been thought at times to be the same species, but are now recognised as separate species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Van_Gogh_-_Stillleben_mit_Makrelen,_Zitronen_und_Tomaten.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2856" data-file-width="3864" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Van_Gogh_-_Stillleben_mit_Makrelen%2C_Zitronen_und_Tomaten.jpeg/220px-Van_Gogh_-_Stillleben_mit_Makrelen%2C_Zitronen_und_Tomaten.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Van_Gogh_-_Stillleben_mit_Makrelen%2C_Zitronen_und_Tomaten.jpeg/330px-Van_Gogh_-_Stillleben_mit_Makrelen%2C_Zitronen_und_Tomaten.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Van_Gogh_-_Stillleben_mit_Makrelen%2C_Zitronen_und_Tomaten.jpeg/440px-Van_Gogh_-_Stillleben_mit_Makrelen%2C_Zitronen_und_Tomaten.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Van_Gogh_-_Stillleben_mit_Makrelen,_Zitronen_und_Tomaten.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Still life with mackerel, lemon and tomato, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Van_Gogh" title="Van Gogh">Van Gogh</a>, 1886</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The term <i>mackerel</i> is also used as a modifier in the common names of other fish, sometimes indicating the fish has vertical stripes similar to a scombroid mackerel:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mackerel_icefish" title="Mackerel icefish">Mackerel icefish</a>—<i>Champsocephalus gunnari</i></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mackerel_pike" title="Mackerel pike">Mackerel pike</a>—<i>Cololabis saira</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mackerel_scad" title="Mackerel scad">Mackerel scad</a>—<i>Decapterus macarellus</i></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mackerel_shark" title="Mackerel shark">Mackerel shark</a>—several species</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sharp-nose_mackerel_shark" title="Sharp-nose mackerel shark">Sharp-nose mackerel shark</a>—<i>Isurus oxyrinchus</i></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mackerel_tuna" title="Mackerel tuna">Mackerel tuna</a>—<i>Euthynnus affinis</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tamasaba" title="Tamasaba">Mackerel tail goldfish</a>—<i>Carassius auratus</i></li>
</ul>
<p>By extension, the term is applied also to other species such as the <a href="/wiki/Tabby_cat#Mackerel_tabby" title="Tabby cat">mackerel tabby cat</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> and to inanimate objects such as the altocumulus <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Altocumulus_mackerel_sky" title="Altocumulus mackerel sky">mackerel sky</a> cloud formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mackerel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scomber_scombrus_illustration.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="338" data-file-width="1039" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Scomber_scombrus_illustration.png/400px-Scomber_scombrus_illustration.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Scomber_scombrus_illustration.png/600px-Scomber_scombrus_illustration.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Scomber_scombrus_illustration.png/800px-Scomber_scombrus_illustration.png 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scomber_scombrus_illustration.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Like other scombroids, mackerel such as this <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_mackerel" title="Atlantic mackerel">Atlantic mackerel</a> are superb swimmers, and can retract their fins into grooves on their bodies for streamlining. They have deeply forked tails and are smaller and slimmer than <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOTuna_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAOTuna-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most mackerel belong to the family <a href="/wiki/Scombridae" title="Scombridae">Scombridae</a>, which also includes <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bonito" title="Bonito">bonito</a>. Generally mackerel are much smaller and slimmer than tuna, though in other respects they share many common characteristics. Their <a href="/wiki/Fish_scale" title="Fish scale">scales</a>, if present at all, are extremely small. Like tuna and bonito, mackerel are voracious feeders, and are swift and manoeuvrable swimmers, able to streamline themselves by retracting their fins into grooves on their body. Like other scombroids, their bodies are cylindrical with numerous <a href="/wiki/Fin" title="Fin">finlets</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dorsum_(biology)" title="Dorsum (biology)">dorsal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ventral" title="Ventral">ventral</a> sides behind the <a href="/wiki/Dorsal_fin" title="Dorsal fin">dorsal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anal_fin" title="Anal fin">anal fins</a>, but unlike the deep-bodied <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a>, they are slim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOTuna_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAOTuna-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Type_species" title="Type species">type species</a> for scombroid mackerels is the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_mackerel" title="Atlantic mackerel">Atlantic mackerel</a>, <i>Scomber scombrus</i>. These fish are iridescent blue-green above with a silvery underbelly and twenty to thirty near vertical wavy black stripes running across their upper body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FishBasescombrus_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-FishBasescombrus-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>It might seem that the prominent stripes on the back of mackerels are there to provide camouflage against broken backgrounds. That is not the case, because mackerel live in midwater pelagic environments which have no background.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denton1998_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Denton1998-117">[117]</a></sup> However, fish have an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optokinetic_reflex" title="Optokinetic reflex">optokinetic reflex</a> in their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vision_in_fish" title="Vision in fish">visual systems</a> which can be sensitive to moving stripes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> In order for fish to school efficiently, they need feedback mechanisms that help them align themselves with adjacent fish, and match their speed. The stripes on neighbouring fish provide "schooling marks" which signal changes in relative position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denton1998_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Denton1998-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bone_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bone-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pacific_Jack_Mackerel_School,_2007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1779" data-file-width="2668" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Pacific_Jack_Mackerel_School%2C_2007.jpg/360px-Pacific_Jack_Mackerel_School%2C_2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Pacific_Jack_Mackerel_School%2C_2007.jpg/540px-Pacific_Jack_Mackerel_School%2C_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Pacific_Jack_Mackerel_School%2C_2007.jpg/720px-Pacific_Jack_Mackerel_School%2C_2007.jpg 2x" width="360"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pacific_Jack_Mackerel_School,_2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mackerel, such as these <a href="/wiki/Pacific_jack_mackerel" title="Pacific jack mackerel">Pacific jack mackerel</a>, usually have vertical stripes on their sides which provide "schooling marks", visual clues that help them stay in formation as they <a href="/wiki/Shoaling_and_schooling" title="Shoaling and schooling">school</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denton1998_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-Denton1998-117">[117]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There is a layer of thin reflecting platelets on some of the mackerel stripes. In 1998, <a href="/wiki/Eric_James_Denton" title="Eric James Denton">E J Denton</a> and D M Rowe argued that these platelets transmit additional information to other fish about how a given fish moves. As the orientation of the fish changes relative to another fish, the amount of light reflected to the second fish by this layer also changes. This sensitivity to orientation gives the mackerel "considerable advantages in being able to react quickly while schooling and feeding."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mackerel range in size from small <a href="/wiki/Forage_fish" title="Forage fish">forage fish</a> to larger <a href="/wiki/Game_fish" title="Game fish">game fish</a>. Coastal mackerel tend to be small.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lal8_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lal8-121">[121]</a></sup> The king mackerel is an example of a larger mackerel. Most fish are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ectothermic" title="Ectothermic">cold-blooded</a>, but there are exceptions. Certain species of fish maintain elevated body temperatures. <a href="/wiki/Warm-blooded" title="Warm-blooded">Endothermic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bony_fish" title="Bony fish">bony fishes</a> are all in the suborder <a href="/wiki/Scombroidei" title="Scombroidei">Scombroidei</a> and include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Butterfly_mackerel" title="Butterfly mackerel">butterfly mackerel</a>, a species of primitive mackerel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mackerel are strong swimmers. Atlantic mackerel can swim at a sustained speed of 0.98 metres/sec with a burst speed of 5.5 m/s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> while chub mackerel can swim at a sustained speed of 0.92 m/s with a burst speed of 2.25 m/s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOTuna_114-2"><a href="#cite_note-FAOTuna-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution">Distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mackerel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:238px;max-width:238px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:236px;max-width:236px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sccav_u0.gif"><img alt="" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Sccav_u0.gif/234px-Sccav_u0.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Sccav_u0.gif/351px-Sccav_u0.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Sccav_u0.gif/468px-Sccav_u0.gif 2x" width="234"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
<center><a href="/wiki/King_mackerel" title="King mackerel">King mackerels</a> cruise on long migrations at 10 kilometres per hour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pfa_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-pfa-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mackerel_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mackerel-126">[126]</a></sup></center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most mackerel species have restricted distribution ranges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOTuna_114-3"><a href="#cite_note-FAOTuna-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Spanish_mackerel" title="Atlantic Spanish mackerel">Atlantic Spanish mackerel</a> <i>(Scomberomorus maculatus)</i> occupy the waters off the east coast of North America from the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Cod" title="Cape Cod">Cape Cod</a> area south to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yucatan_Peninsula" title="Yucatan Peninsula">Yucatan Peninsula</a>. Its population is considered to include two <a href="/wiki/Fish_stock" title="Fish stock">fish stocks</a>, defined by geography. As summer approaches, one stock moves in large schools north from Florida up the coast to spawn in shallow waters off the <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> coast. It then returns to winter in deeper waters off Florida. The other stock migrates in large schools along the coast from Mexico to spawn in shallow waters of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> off Texas. It then returns to winter in deeper waters off the Mexican coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOmaculatus_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAOmaculatus-56">[56]</a></sup> These stocks are managed separately, even though genetically they are identical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCNmaculatus_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-IUCNmaculatus-57">[57]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_mackerel" title="Atlantic mackerel">Atlantic mackerel</a> <i>(Scomber scombrus)</i> is a coastal species found only in the north Atlantic. The stock on the west side of the Atlantic is largely independent of the stock on the east side. The stock on the east Atlantic currently operates as three separate stocks, the southern, western and North Sea stocks, each with their own migration patterns. Some mixing of the east Atlantic stocks takes place in feeding grounds towards the north, but there is almost no mixing between the east and west Atlantic stocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCNscombrus_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-IUCNscombrus-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></li>
<li>Another common coastal species, the <a href="/wiki/Chub_mackerel" title="Chub mackerel">chub mackerel</a> <i>(Scomber japonicus)</i>, is absent from the Atlantic Ocean but is widespread across both hemispheres in the Pacific, where its migration patterns are somewhat similar to those of Atlantic mackerel. In the northern hemisphere, chub mackerel migrate northwards in the summer to feeding grounds, and southwards in the winter when they spawn in relatively shallow waters. In the southern hemisphere the migrations are reversed. After spawning, some stocks migrate down the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_slope" title="Continental slope">continental slope</a> to deeper water and spend the rest of the winter in relative inactivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FishBasejaponicus_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-FishBasejaponicus-23">[23]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Chilean_jack_mackerel" title="Chilean jack mackerel">Chilean jack mackerel</a> (<i>Trachurus murphyi</i>), the most intensively harvested mackerel-like species, is found in the south Pacific from West Australia to the coasts of Chile and Peru.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FishBasemurphyi_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-FishBasemurphyi-89">[89]</a></sup> A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sister_species" title="Sister species">sister species</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_jack_mackerel" title="Pacific jack mackerel">Pacific jack mackerel</a> (<i>Trachurus symmetricus</i>), is found in the north Pacific. The Chilean jack mackerel occurs along the coasts in <a href="/wiki/Upwelling" title="Upwelling">upwelling</a> areas, but also migrates across the open ocean. Its <a href="/wiki/Abundance_(ecology)" title="Abundance (ecology)">abundance</a> can fluctuate markedly as ocean conditions change,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCNmurphyi_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-IUCNmurphyi-91">[91]</a></sup> and is particularly affected by the <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Niño">El Niño</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p>Three species of jack mackerels are found in coastal waters around New Zealand: the <a href="/wiki/Greenback_horse_mackerel" title="Greenback horse mackerel">Australasian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chilean_jack_mackerel" title="Chilean jack mackerel">Chilean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pacific_jack_mackerel" title="Pacific jack mackerel">Pacific jack mackerels</a>. They are mainly captured using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Purse_seine" title="Purse seine">purse seine</a> nets, and are managed as a single stock that includes multiple species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some mackerel species migrate vertically. Adult <a href="/wiki/Snake_mackerel" title="Snake mackerel">snake mackerels</a> conduct a <a href="/wiki/Diel_vertical_migration" title="Diel vertical migration">diel vertical migration</a>, staying in deeper water during the day and rising to the surface at night to feed. The young and juveniles also migrate vertically but in the opposite direction, staying near the surface during the day and moving deeper at night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burton_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-burton-132">[132]</a></sup> This species feeds on squid, pelagic <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lanternfish" title="Lanternfish">lanternfishes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flying_fish" title="Flying fish">flying fishes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sauries" title="Sauries">sauries</a> and other mackerel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mceachran_and_fechhelm_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-mceachran_and_fechhelm-133">[133]</a></sup> It is in turn preyed upon by tuna and marlin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peterson_et_al_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-peterson_et_al-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_cycle">Life cycle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mackerel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Life cycle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scramble%5E%5E_A_Gannet_begins_its_take_off_run._-_geograph.org.uk_-_1368991.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="486" data-file-width="640" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Scramble%5E%5E_A_Gannet_begins_its_take_off_run._-_geograph.org.uk_-_1368991.jpg/220px-Scramble%5E%5E_A_Gannet_begins_its_take_off_run._-_geograph.org.uk_-_1368991.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Scramble%5E%5E_A_Gannet_begins_its_take_off_run._-_geograph.org.uk_-_1368991.jpg/330px-Scramble%5E%5E_A_Gannet_begins_its_take_off_run._-_geograph.org.uk_-_1368991.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Scramble%5E%5E_A_Gannet_begins_its_take_off_run._-_geograph.org.uk_-_1368991.jpg/440px-Scramble%5E%5E_A_Gannet_begins_its_take_off_run._-_geograph.org.uk_-_1368991.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scramble%5E%5E_A_Gannet_begins_its_take_off_run._-_geograph.org.uk_-_1368991.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Gannet" title="Gannet">Gannets</a> and other seabirds fuel themselves with mackerel</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mackerel are prolific <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broadcast_spawner" title="Broadcast spawner">broadcast spawners</a> and must breed near the surface of the water due to the eggs of the females floating. Individual females lay between 300,000 and 1,500,000 eggs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOTuna_114-4"><a href="#cite_note-FAOTuna-114">[114]</a></sup> Their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fish_egg" title="Fish egg">eggs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fish_larvae" title="Fish larvae">larvae</a> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pelagic" title="Pelagic">pelagic</a>, that is, they float free in the open sea. The larvae and juvenile mackerel feed on <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a>. As adults they have sharp teeth, and hunt small crustaceans such as <a href="/wiki/Copepod" title="Copepod">copepods</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Forage_fish" title="Forage fish">forage fish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shrimp" title="Shrimp">shrimp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a>. In turn they are hunted by larger pelagic animals such as <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Billfish" title="Billfish">billfish</a>, sea lions, <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pelican" title="Pelican">pelicans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOjaponicus_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAOjaponicus-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOcavalla_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAOcavalla-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>Off Madagascar, spinner sharks follow migrating schools of mackerel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-compagno_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-compagno-136">[136]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bryde%27s_whale" title="Bryde's whale">Bryde's whales</a> feed on mackerel when they can find them. They use several feeding methods, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ram_feeding" title="Ram feeding">skimming the surface</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunge_feeding" title="Lunge feeding">lunging</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bubble_net" title="Bubble net">bubble nets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fisheries">Fisheries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mackerel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Fisheries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:404px;max-width:404px">
<div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:;background-color:">Global capture of mackerel in tonnes reported by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FAO" title="FAO">FAO</a> 1950–2009</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:402px;max-width:402px">

</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:402px;max-width:402px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_capture_of_other_mackerel_1950%E2%80%932009.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="736" data-file-width="1180" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Global_capture_of_other_mackerel_1950%E2%80%932009.png/400px-Global_capture_of_other_mackerel_1950%E2%80%932009.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Global_capture_of_other_mackerel_1950%E2%80%932009.png/600px-Global_capture_of_other_mackerel_1950%E2%80%932009.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Global_capture_of_other_mackerel_1950%E2%80%932009.png/800px-Global_capture_of_other_mackerel_1950%E2%80%932009.png 2x" width="400"/></a></div>

</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
<div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:">Main commercial species</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scjap_u0.gif"><img alt="" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Scjap_u0.gif/220px-Scjap_u0.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Scjap_u0.gif/330px-Scjap_u0.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Scjap_u0.gif/440px-Scjap_u0.gif 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
<center>The <a href="/wiki/Chub_mackerel" title="Chub mackerel">chub mackerel</a> is the most intensively fished mackerel in the scombroid family</center>
</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chilean_purse_seine.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1191" data-file-width="1767" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Chilean_purse_seine.jpg/220px-Chilean_purse_seine.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Chilean_purse_seine.jpg/330px-Chilean_purse_seine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Chilean_purse_seine.jpg/440px-Chilean_purse_seine.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Chilean_jack_mackerel" title="Chilean jack mackerel">Chilean jack mackerel</a> have been overfished and may be in danger of collapsing. Here an entire <a href="/wiki/Shoaling_and_schooling" title="Shoaling and schooling">school</a> of about 400 tons is encircled by a purse seiner.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chub_mackerel" title="Chub mackerel">Chub mackerel</a>, <i>Scomber japonicus</i>, are the most intensively fished scombroid mackerel. As can be seen from the graph on the right, they account for about half the total capture production of scombroid mackerels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOdata_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-FAOdata-1">[1]</a></sup> As a species they are easily confused with Atlantic mackerel. Chub mackerel migrate long distances in oceans and across the Mediterranean. They can be caught with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drift_net" title="Drift net">drift nets</a> and suitable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trawl" title="Trawl">trawls</a>, but are most usually caught with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surround_net" title="Surround net">surround nets</a> at night by attracting them with lampara lamps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>The remaining catch of scombroid mackerels is divided equally between the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_mackerel" title="Atlantic mackerel">Atlantic mackerel</a> and all other scombroid mackerels. Just two species account for about 75% of the total catch of scombroid mackerels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOdata_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-FAOdata-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chilean_jack_mackerel" title="Chilean jack mackerel">Chilean jack mackerel</a> are the most commonly fished non-scombroid mackerel, fished as heavily as chub mackerel<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOdata_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-FAOdata-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOmurphyi_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAOmurphyi-90">[90]</a></sup> (see second graph on the right). The species has been <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfished</a>, and its fishery may now be in danger of collapsing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iWatch2_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-iWatch2-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>Smaller mackerel behave like <a href="/wiki/Herring" title="Herring">herrings</a>, and are captured in similar ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clapham1941_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clapham1941-141">[141]</a></sup> Fish species like these, which <a href="/wiki/Shoaling_and_schooling" title="Shoaling and schooling">school</a> near the surface, can be caught efficiently by <a href="/wiki/Seine_fishing" title="Seine fishing">purse seining</a>. Huge purse seiner vessels use spotter planes to locate the schooling fish. Then they close in using sophisticated sonar to track the shape of the shoal. Entire schools are then encircled with fast auxiliary boats which deploy purse seine nets as they speed around the school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>Suitably designed <a href="/wiki/Trolling_(fishing)" title="Trolling (fishing)">trollers</a> can also catch mackerels effectively when they swim near the surface. Trollers typically have several long booms which they lift and drop with "topping lifts". They haul their lines with electric or hydraulic reels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fish_aggregating_device" title="Fish aggregating device">Fish aggregating devices</a> are also used to target mackerel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hi_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-hi-145">[145]</a></sup></p>

</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:195px;max-width:195px">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Management">Management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mackerel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> has been <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfished</a> to the point where the ecological balance has become disrupted and many jobs in the fishing industry have been lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Southeast US region spans the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean Sea</a> and the US Southeast Atlantic. Overfishing of <a href="/wiki/King_mackerel" title="King mackerel">king</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Spanish_mackerel" title="Atlantic Spanish mackerel">Spanish mackerel</a> occurred in the 1980s. Regulations were introduced to restrict the size, amount of catch, fishing locations and bag limits for recreational fishers as well as commercial fishers. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gillnet" title="Gillnet">Gillnets</a> were banned in waters off Florida. By 2001, the mackerel stocks had bounced back.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAO_Profile_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_Profile-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="As_food">As food</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mackerel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: As food">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maquereaux_etal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="525" data-file-width="700" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Maquereaux_etal.jpg/220px-Maquereaux_etal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Maquereaux_etal.jpg/330px-Maquereaux_etal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Maquereaux_etal.jpg/440px-Maquereaux_etal.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maquereaux_etal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center>Atlantic mackerel on ice at a fishmongers</center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mackerel_as_food" title="Mackerel as food">Mackerel as food</a></div>
<p>Mackerel is an important <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_fish" title="Food fish">food fish</a> that is consumed worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> As an <a href="/wiki/Oily_fish" title="Oily fish">oily fish</a>, it is a rich source of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omega-3_fatty_acids" title="Omega-3 fatty acids">omega-3 fatty acids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> The flesh of mackerel spoils quickly, especially in the tropics, and can cause <a href="/wiki/Scombroid_food_poisoning" title="Scombroid food poisoning">scombroid food poisoning</a>. Accordingly, it should be eaten on the day of capture, unless properly refrigerated or <a href="/wiki/Curing_(food_preservation)" title="Curing (food preservation)">cured</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mackerel preservation is not simple. Before the 19th-century development of canning and the widespread availability of refrigeration, salting and smoking were the principal preservation methods available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> Historically in England, this fish was not preserved, but was consumed only in its fresh form. However, spoilage was common, leading the authors of <i>The Cambridge Economic History of Europe</i> to remark: "There are more references to stinking mackerel in English literature than to any other fish!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clapham1941_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clapham1941-141">[141]</a></sup> In France mackerel was traditionally pickled with large amounts of salt, which allowed it to be sold widely across the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clapham1941_141-2"><a href="#cite_note-Clapham1941-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mackerel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
